	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Belief</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">&#160;&#160;(Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief_systems&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-redirect" title="Belief systems">Belief systems</a>)</span></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the general concept. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Belief_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Belief (disambiguation)">Belief (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Psychological state in which an individual holds a proposition or premise to be true</div>

<p>Related concepts and fundamentals:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Agnosticism" title="Agnosticism">Agnosticism</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">Epistemology</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Presupposition_(philosophy)" title="Presupposition (philosophy)">Presupposition</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Probability" title="Probability">Probability</a></li></ul></td>
</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Certainty_sidebar" title="Template:Certainty sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Certainty_sidebar" title="Template talk:Certainty sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Certainty_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Belief</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Attitude_(psychology)" title="Attitude (psychology)">attitude</a> that something is the case or <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">true</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a>, philosophers use the term "belief" to refer to personal attitudes associated with <a href="/wiki/Truth_value" title="Truth value">true or false</a> ideas and concepts. However, "belief" does not require active <a href="/wiki/Introspection" title="Introspection">introspection</a> and circumspection. For example, few ponder whether the sun will rise, just assume it will. Since "belief" is an important aspect of mundane life, according to <a href="/wiki/Eric_Schwitzgebel" title="Eric Schwitzgebel">Eric Schwitzgebel</a> in the <i>Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy</i>, a related question asks: "how a physical organism can have beliefs?"<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the context of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy" title="Ancient Greek philosophy">Ancient Greek thought</a>, two related concepts were identified regarding the concept of belief: <i><a href="/wiki/Pistis#Pistis_in_rhetoric" title="Pistis">pistis</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Doxa" title="Doxa">doxa</a></i>. Simplified, <i>pistis</i> refers to "trust" and "confidence", while <i>doxa</i> refers to "opinion" and "acceptance".  The English word "<a href="/wiki/Orthodoxy" title="Orthodoxy">orthodoxy</a>" derives from <i>doxa</i>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Psychology">Psychology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Psychology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Mainstream psychology and related disciplines have traditionally treated belief as if it were the simplest form of mental representation and therefore one of the building blocks of conscious thought. Philosophers have tended to be more abstract in their analysis, and much of the work examining the viability of the belief concept stems from philosophical analysis.
</p><p>The concept of belief presumes a subject (the believer) and an object of belief (the proposition). So, like other <a href="/wiki/Propositional_attitude" title="Propositional attitude">propositional attitudes</a>, belief implies the existence of <a href="/wiki/Mental_state" title="Mental state">mental states</a> and <a href="/wiki/Intentionality" title="Intentionality">intentionality</a>, both of which are hotly debated topics in the <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mind" title="Philosophy of mind">philosophy of mind</a>, whose foundations and relation to brain states are still controversial.
</p><p>Beliefs are sometimes divided into <a href="/wiki/Core_beliefs" class="mw-redirect" title="Core beliefs">core beliefs</a> (that are actively thought about) and <a href="/wiki/Dispositional_belief" class="mw-redirect" title="Dispositional belief">dispositional beliefs</a> (that may be ascribed to someone who has not thought about the issue). For example, if asked "do you believe tigers wear pink pajamas?" a person might answer that they do not, despite the fact they may never have thought about this situation before.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This has important implications for understanding the <a href="/wiki/Neuropsychology" title="Neuropsychology">neuropsychology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neuroscience" title="Neuroscience">neuroscience</a> of belief. If the concept of belief is incoherent, then any attempt to find the underlying neural processes that support it will fail.
</p><p>Philosopher <a href="/wiki/Lynne_Rudder_Baker" title="Lynne Rudder Baker">Lynne Rudder Baker</a> has outlined four main contemporary approaches to belief in her controversial book <i>Saving Belief</i>:<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Our common-sense understanding of belief is correct – Sometimes called the "mental sentence theory," in this conception, beliefs exist as coherent entities, and the way we talk about them in everyday life is a valid basis for scientific endeavour. <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Fodor" title="Jerry Fodor">Jerry Fodor</a> was one of the principal defenders of this point of view.</li>
<li>Our common-sense understanding of belief may not be entirely correct, but it is close enough to make some useful predictions – This view argues that we will eventually reject the idea of belief as we know it now, but that there may be a correlation between what we take to be a belief when someone says "I believe that snow is white" and how a future theory of psychology will explain this behaviour. Most notably, philosopher <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Stich" title="Stephen Stich">Stephen Stich</a> has argued for this particular understanding of belief.</li>
<li>Our common-sense understanding of belief is entirely wrong and will be completely superseded by a radically different theory that will have no use for the concept of belief as we know it – Known as <a href="/wiki/Eliminativism" class="mw-redirect" title="Eliminativism">eliminativism</a>, this view (most notably proposed by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Churchland" title="Paul Churchland">Paul</a> and <a href="/wiki/Patricia_Churchland" title="Patricia Churchland">Patricia Churchland</a>) argues that the concept of belief is like obsolete theories of times past such as <a href="/wiki/The_four_humours" class="mw-redirect" title="The four humours">the four humours</a> theory of medicine, or the <a href="/wiki/Phlogiston_theory" title="Phlogiston theory">phlogiston theory</a> of combustion. In these cases science hasn't provided us with a more detailed account of these theories, but completely rejected them as valid scientific concepts to be replaced by entirely different accounts. The Churchlands argue that our common-sense concept of belief is similar in that as we discover more about neuroscience and the brain, the inevitable conclusion will be to reject the belief hypothesis in its entirety.</li>
<li>Our common-sense understanding of belief is entirely wrong; however, treating people, animals, and even computers as if they had beliefs is often a successful strategy – The major proponents of this view, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Dennett" title="Daniel Dennett">Daniel Dennett</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lynne_Rudder_Baker" title="Lynne Rudder Baker">Lynne Rudder Baker</a>, are both <a href="/wiki/Eliminativism" class="mw-redirect" title="Eliminativism">eliminativists</a> in that they hold that beliefs are not a scientifically valid concept, but they don't go as far as rejecting the concept of belief as a predictive device. Dennett gives the example of playing a computer at chess. While few people would agree that the computer held beliefs, treating the computer as if it did (e.g. that the computer believes that taking the opposition's queen will give it a considerable advantage) is likely to be a successful and predictive strategy. In this understanding of belief, named by Dennett <i>the <a href="/wiki/Intentional_stance" title="Intentional stance">intentional stance</a></i>, belief-based explanations of mind and behaviour are at a different level of explanation and are not reducible to those based on fundamental neuroscience, although both may be explanatory at their own level.</li></ul>
<p>Strategic approaches make a distinction between rules, norms and beliefs as follows:
(1) Rules. Explicit regulative processes such as policies, laws, inspection routines, or incentives. Rules function as a coercive regulator of behavior and are dependent upon the imposing entity's ability to enforce them.
(2) Norms. Regulative mechanisms accepted by the social collective. Norms are enforced by normative mechanisms within the organization and are not strictly dependent upon law or regulation.
(3) Beliefs. The collective perception of fundamental truths governing behavior. The adherence to accepted and shared beliefs by members of a social system will likely persist and be difficult to change over time. Strong beliefs about determinant factors (i.e., security, survival, or honor) are likely to cause a social entity or group to accept rules and norms.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Knowledge_and_epistemology">Knowledge and epistemology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Knowledge and epistemology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:238px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Classical_definition_of_Kno.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Classical_definition_of_Kno.svg/236px-Classical_definition_of_Kno.svg.png" decoding="async" width="236" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Classical_definition_of_Kno.svg/354px-Classical_definition_of_Kno.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Classical_definition_of_Kno.svg/472px-Classical_definition_of_Kno.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="342" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Classical_definition_of_Kno.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Venn_diagram" title="Venn diagram">Venn</a>/<a href="/wiki/Euler_diagram" title="Euler diagram">Euler diagram</a> which grants that <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">truth</a> and well-justified belief may be distinguished and that their <a href="/wiki/Intersection_(set_theory)" title="Intersection (set theory)">intersection</a> is <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">Epistemology</a> is concerned with delineating the boundary between justified belief and <a href="/wiki/Opinion" title="Opinion">opinion</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> and involved generally with a theoretical <a href="/wiki/Philosophical" class="mw-redirect" title="Philosophical">philosophical</a> study of <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a>. The primary problem in epistemology is to understand exactly what is needed in order for us to have knowledge. In a notion derived from <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>'s dialogue <i><a href="/wiki/Theaetetus_(dialogue)" title="Theaetetus (dialogue)">Theaetetus</a></i>, where the epistemology of Socrates (Platon) most clearly departs from that of the <a href="/wiki/Sophist" title="Sophist">sophists</a>, who at the time of Plato seem to have defined knowledge as what is here expressed as "<a href="/wiki/Justified_true_belief" class="mw-redirect" title="Justified true belief">justified true belief</a>". The tendency to translate from belief (here: doxa – common opinion) to knowledge (here: episteme), which Plato (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a> of the dialogue) utterly dismisses, results from failing to distinguish a <a href="/wiki/Dispositive" class="mw-redirect" title="Dispositive">dispositive</a> belief (gr. 'doxa', not 'pistis') from knowledge (episteme) when the opinion is regarded <i>true</i> (here: orthé), in terms of right, and juristically so (according to the premises of the dialogue), which was the task of the <a href="/wiki/Rhetor" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhetor">rhetors</a> to prove. Plato dismisses this possibility of an affirmative relation between belief (i.e. opinion) and <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a> even when the one who opines grounds his belief on the rule, and is able to add <i>justification</i> (gr. <a href="/wiki/Logos" title="Logos">logos</a>: reasonable and necessarily plausible assertions/evidence/guidance) to it.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Plato has been credited for the "<i>justified true belief</i>" theory of knowledge, even though Plato in the <a href="/wiki/Theaetetus_(dialogue)" title="Theaetetus (dialogue)">Theaetetus (dialogue)</a> elegantly dismisses it, and even posits this argument of Socrates as a cause for his death penalty. Among American epistemologists, <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Gettier" title="Edmund Gettier">Gettier</a> (1963)<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Alvin_Goldman" title="Alvin Goldman">Goldman</a> (1967),<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> have questioned the "justified true belief" definition, and challenged the "sophists" of their time.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Justified_true_belief">Justified true belief</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Justified true belief">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><b>Justified true belief</b> is a definition of <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a> that gained approval during the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a>, 'justified' standing in contrast to 'revealed'. There have been attempts to trace it back to <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> and his dialogues.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (June 2017)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The concept of justified true belief states that in order to know that a given proposition is true, one must not only believe the relevant true proposition, but also have justification for doing so. In more formal terms, an agent <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle S}">

</p>
