<p>This theory of knowledge suffered a significant setback with the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Gettier_problem" title="Gettier problem">Gettier problems</a>, situations in which the above conditions were seemingly met but where many philosophers disagree that anything is known.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>  <a href="/wiki/Robert_Nozick" title="Robert Nozick">Robert Nozick</a> suggested a <a href="/wiki/Gettier_problems#Fred_Dretske&#39;s_conclusive_reasons_and_Robert_Nozick&#39;s_truth-tracking" class="mw-redirect" title="Gettier problems">clarification</a> of "justification" which he believed eliminates the problem: the justification has to be such that were the justification false, the knowledge would be false.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Bernecker and Dretske (2000) argue that "no epistemologist since Gettier has seriously and successfully defended the traditional view."<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>3</span></sup>  On the other hand, Paul Boghossian argues that the Justified True Belief account is the "standard, widely accepted" definition of knowledge.<sup id="cite_ref-Boghossian_on_Justification_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boghossian_on_Justification-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">Formation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Formation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ripples_of_influence.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Ripples_of_influence.jpg/220px-Ripples_of_influence.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Ripples_of_influence.jpg/330px-Ripples_of_influence.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Ripples_of_influence.jpg/440px-Ripples_of_influence.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3888" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ripples_of_influence.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>We are influenced by many factors that ripple through our minds as our beliefs form, evolve, and may eventually change</div></div></div>
<p>Psychologists study belief formation and the relationship between beliefs and actions. Three models of belief formation and change have been proposed:
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conditional_inference_process">Conditional inference process</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Conditional inference process">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>When people are asked to estimate the likelihood that a statement is true, they search their memory for information that has implications for the validity of this statement. Once this information has been identified, they estimate a) the likelihood that the statement would be true if the information were true, and b) the likelihood that the statement would be true if the information were false. If their estimates for these two <a href="/wiki/Probability" title="Probability">probabilities</a> differ, people average them, weighting each by the likelihood that the information is true and false (respectively). Thus, information bears directly on beliefs of another, related statement.<sup id="cite_ref-Wyer,_R._S._2005_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wyer,_R._S._2005-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Linear_models">Linear models</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Linear models">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Unlike the previous model, this one takes into consideration the possibility of multiple factors influencing belief formation. Using regression procedures, this model predicts belief formation on the basis of several different pieces of information, with weights assigned to each piece on the basis of their relative importance.<sup id="cite_ref-Wyer,_R._S._2005_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wyer,_R._S._2005-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Information_processing_models_and_change">Information processing models and change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Information processing models and change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>These models address the fact that the responses people have to belief-relevant information is unlikely to be predicted from the objective basis of the information that they can recall at the time their beliefs are reported. Instead, these responses reflect the number and meaning of the thoughts that people have about the message at the time that they encounter it.<sup id="cite_ref-Wyer,_R._S._2005_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wyer,_R._S._2005-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some influences on people's belief formation include:
</p>
<ul><li>Internalization of beliefs during childhood, which can form and shape our beliefs in different domains. <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> is often quoted as having said that "Common sense is the collection of prejudices acquired by age eighteen." Political beliefs depend most strongly on the political beliefs most common in the community where we live.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Most individuals believe the <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a> they were taught in childhood.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Charismatic leaders can form and/or modify beliefs (even if those beliefs fly in the face of all previous beliefs).<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Is belief voluntary? Rational individuals need to reconcile their direct reality with any said belief; therefore, if belief is not present or possible, it reflects the fact that contradictions were necessarily overcome using <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_dissonance" title="Cognitive dissonance">cognitive dissonance</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Advertising" title="Advertising">Advertising</a> can form or change beliefs through repetition, shock, and association with images of sex, love, beauty, and other strong positive emotions.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Contrary to intuition, a delay, known as the <a href="/wiki/Sleeper_effect" title="Sleeper effect">sleeper effect</a>, instead of immediate succession may increase an advertisement's ability to persuade viewer's beliefs if a discounting cue is present.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Physical trauma, especially to the head, can radically alter a person's beliefs.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>However, even educated people, well aware of the process by which beliefs form, still strongly cling to their beliefs, and act on those beliefs even against their own self-interest. In Anna Rowley's book, Leadership Therapy, she states "You want your beliefs to change. It's proof that you are keeping your eyes open, living fully, and welcoming everything that the world and people around you can teach you." This means that peoples' beliefs should evolve as they gain new experiences.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Revision">Revision</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Revision">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Belief_revision" title="Belief revision">Belief revision</a></div>
<p>An extensive amount of scientific research and philosophical discussion exists around the modification of beliefs, which is commonly referred to as belief revision.
Generally speaking, the process of belief revision entails the believer weighing the set of truths and/or evidence, and the dominance of a set of truths or evidence on an alternative to a held belief can lead to revision.  One process of belief revision is <a href="/wiki/Bayesian_inference" title="Bayesian inference">Bayesian updating</a> and is often referenced for its mathematical basis and conceptual simplicity.  However, such a process may not be representative for individuals whose beliefs are not easily characterized as probabilistic.
</p><p>There are several techniques for individuals or groups to change the beliefs of others; these methods generally fall under the umbrella of <a href="/wiki/Persuasion" title="Persuasion">persuasion</a>.   Persuasion can take on more specific forms such as  <a href="/wiki/Consciousness_raising" title="Consciousness raising">consciousness raising</a> when considered in an activist or political context.
Belief modification may also occur as a result of the experience of outcomes.  Because <a href="/wiki/Goal" title="Goal">goals</a> are based, in part on beliefs, the success or failure at a particular goal may contribute to modification of beliefs that supported the original goal.
</p><p>Whether or not belief modification actually occurs is dependent not only on the extent of truths or evidence for the alternative belief, but also characteristics outside the specific truths or evidence.  This includes, but is not limited to: the source characteristics of the message, such as <a href="/wiki/Source_credibility" title="Source credibility">credibility</a>; <a href="/wiki/Peer_pressure" title="Peer pressure">social pressures</a>; the anticipated consequences of a modification; or the ability of the individual or group to act on the modification.  Therefore, individuals seeking to achieve belief modification in themselves or others need to consider all possible forms of resistance to belief revision.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Partial">Partial</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Partial">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">April 2019</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Without qualification, "belief" normally implies a lack of <a href="/wiki/Doubt" title="Doubt">doubt</a>, especially insofar as it is a designation of a <a href="/wiki/Life_stance" title="Life stance">life stance</a>. In practical everyday use however, belief is normally partial and retractable with varying degrees of <a href="/wiki/Certainty" title="Certainty">certainty</a>.
</p><p>A copious literature exists in multiple disciplines to accommodate this reality. In mathematics <a href="/wiki/Probability" title="Probability">probability</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fuzzy_logic" title="Fuzzy logic">fuzzy logic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fuzzy_set_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Fuzzy set theory">fuzzy set theory</a>, and other topics are largely directed to this.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prediction">Prediction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Prediction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Different psychological models have tried to predict people's beliefs and some of them try to estimate the exact probabilities of beliefs. For example, <a href="/wiki/Robert_S._Wyer" title="Robert S. Wyer">Robert Wyer</a> developed a model of subjective probabilities.<sup id="cite_ref-Wyer,_R._S._1970_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wyer,_R._S._1970-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WyerGoldberg_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WyerGoldberg-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> When people rate the likelihood of a certain statement (e.g., "It will rain tomorrow"), this rating can be seen as a subjective probability value. The subjective probability model posits that these subjective probabilities follow the same rules as objective probabilities. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_total_probability" title="Law of total probability">law of total probability</a> might be applied to predict a subjective probability value. Wyer found that this model produces relatively accurate predictions for probabilities of single events and for changes in these probabilities, but that the probabilities of several beliefs linked by "and" or "or" do not follow the model as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Wyer,_R._S._1970_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wyer,_R._S._1970-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WyerGoldberg_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WyerGoldberg-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epistemology_versus_religion">Epistemology versus religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Epistemology versus religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Historically <i>belief-in</i> belonged in the realm of religious thought, <i>belief-that</i> instead belonged to epistemological considerations.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Belief-in">Belief-in</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Belief-in">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>To "believe in" someone or something is a distinct concept from "believing-that." There are at least these types of belief-in:<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><b>Commendatory</b> / <b><a href="/wiki/Faith" title="Faith">Faith</a></b> – we may make an expression of 'faith' in respect of some performance by an agent X, when without prejudice to the truth value of the factual outcome or even confidence in X otherwise, we expect that specific performance. In particular <a href="/wiki/Self-confidence" title="Self-confidence">self-confidence</a> or faith in one's self is this kind of belief.</li>
<li><b>Existential claim</b> – to claim belief in the existence of an entity or phenomenon in a general way with the implied need to justify its claim of existence. It is often used when the entity is not real, or its existence is in doubt. "He believes in witches and ghosts" or "many children believe in <a href="/wiki/Santa_Claus" title="Santa Claus">Santa Claus</a>" or "I believe in a deity" are typical examples.<sup id="cite_ref-belief-in_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-belief-in-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The linguistic form is distinct from the assertion of the truth of a proposition since verification is either considered impossible/irrelevant or a <a href="/wiki/Counterfactual" class="mw-redirect" title="Counterfactual">counterfactual</a> situation is assumed.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Belief-that">Belief-that</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Belief-that">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_belief">Economic belief</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Economic belief">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Economic_ideology" title="Economic ideology">Economic ideology</a></div>
<p>Economic beliefs are beliefs which are reasonably and necessarily contrary to the tenet of rational choice or instrumental rationality.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Studies of the Austrian tradition of the economic thought, in the context of analysis of the influence and subsequent degree of change resulting from  existing economic knowledge and belief, has contributed the most to the subsequent holistic collective analysis.<sup id="cite_ref-R._Arena_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-R._Arena-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Delusion">Delusion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Delusion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Insofar as the truth of belief is expressed in sentential  and propositional form we are using the sense of <i>belief-that</i> rather than <i>belief-in</i>. <a href="/wiki/Delusion" title="Delusion">Delusion</a> arises when the truth value of the form is clearly nil.<sup id="cite_ref-L._Bortolotti_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L._Bortolotti-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Delusions are defined as beliefs in <a href="/wiki/Psychiatric" class="mw-redirect" title="Psychiatric">psychiatric</a> diagnostic criteria<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> (for example in the <i><a href="/wiki/Diagnostic_and_Statistical_Manual_of_Mental_Disorders" title="Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders">Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders</a></i>). Psychiatrist and historian <a href="/wiki/G.E._Berrios" class="mw-redirect" title="G.E. Berrios">G.E. Berrios</a> has challenged the view that delusions are genuine beliefs and instead labels them as "empty speech acts," where affected persons are motivated to express false or bizarre belief statements due to an underlying psychological disturbance. However, the majority of mental health professionals and researchers treat delusions as if they were genuine beliefs.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Carroll" title="Lewis Carroll">Lewis Carroll</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Through_the_Looking-Glass" title="Through the Looking-Glass">Through the Looking-Glass</a></i> the <a href="/wiki/White_Queen_(Through_the_Looking-Glass)" title="White Queen (Through the Looking-Glass)">White Queen</a> says, "Why, sometimes I've believed as many as six impossible things before breakfast." This is often quoted in mockery of the common ability of people to <a href="/wiki/Credo_quia_absurdum" title="Credo quia absurdum">entertain beliefs contrary to fact</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Science">Science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In scientific <a href="/wiki/Medical_writing" title="Medical writing">medical writing</a>, the verb 'believe' can mean "actively accept as true" on the basis of external <a href="/wiki/Scientific_evidence" title="Scientific evidence">evidence</a> (for example, a statement of the type, "we believe that <i>x</i> is a better treatment than <i>y</i> in this disease" can imply that "after examining the available evidence, we have concluded that <i>x</i> is...").<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Religion_collage_updated.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Religion collage updated.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Religion_collage_updated.jpg/220px-Religion_collage_updated.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Religion_collage_updated.jpg/330px-Religion_collage_updated.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Religion_collage_updated.jpg/440px-Religion_collage_updated.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2017" data-file-height="1382" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Religion_collage_updated.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div></div></div></div>
<p>Religious belief refers to attitudes towards <a href="/wiki/Mythological" class="mw-redirect" title="Mythological">mythological</a>, <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Spirituality" title="Spirituality">spiritual</a> aspects of a <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Religious belief is distinct from <a href="/wiki/Religious_ritual" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious ritual">religious practice</a> and from <a href="/wiki/Religious_behaviour" title="Religious behaviour">religious behaviours</a> – with some believers not practicing <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a> and some practitioners not believing religion. Religious beliefs, deriving from ideas that are exclusive to religion,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> often relate to the existence, characteristics and worship of a <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">deity</a> or deities, to the idea of <a href="/wiki/Miracle" title="Miracle">divine intervention</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Personal_life" title="Personal life">human life</a>, or to the <a href="/wiki/Deontological" class="mw-redirect" title="Deontological">deontological</a>  explanations for the values and practices centered on the teachings of a <a href="/wiki/Spiritual_leader" class="mw-redirect" title="Spiritual leader">spiritual leader</a> or of a <a href="/w/index.php?title=Spiritual_group&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Spiritual group (page does not exist)">spiritual group</a>. In contrast to other <a href="/wiki/Belief_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Belief system">belief systems</a>, religious beliefs are usually <a href="/wiki/Codification_(law)" title="Codification (law)">codified</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Forms">Forms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Forms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A popular view holds that different religions each have identifiable and exclusive sets of beliefs or <a href="/wiki/Creed" title="Creed">creeds</a>, but surveys of religious belief have often found that the official doctrine and descriptions of the beliefs offered by <a href="/wiki/Religious_authority" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious authority">religious authorities</a> do not always agree with the privately held beliefs of those who identify as members of a particular religion.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> For a broad classification of the kinds of religious belief, see below.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fundamentalism">Fundamentalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Fundamentalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religious_fundamentalism" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious fundamentalism">Religious fundamentalism</a></div>
<p>First self-applied as a term to the conservative doctrine outlined by anti-modernist <a href="/wiki/Protestants" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestants">Protestants</a> in the United States,<sup id="cite_ref-A.C._Dixon_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A.C._Dixon-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> "fundamentalism" in religious terms denotes strict adherence to an interpretation of scriptures that are generally associated with theologically conservative positions or traditional understandings of the text and are distrustful of innovative readings, new revelation, or alternative interpretations.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Religious fundamentalism has been identified<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (November 2017)">by whom?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> in the media as being associated with <a href="/wiki/Fanaticism" title="Fanaticism">fanatical</a> or <a href="/wiki/Zealotry" class="mw-redirect" title="Zealotry">zealous</a> political movements around the world that have used a strict adherence to a particular religious doctrine as a means to establish political identity and to enforce societal norms.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Orthodoxy">Orthodoxy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Orthodoxy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Orthodoxy" title="Orthodoxy">Orthodoxy</a></div>
<p>First used in the context of <a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">Early Christianity</a>, the term "orthodoxy" relates to religious belief that closely follows the edicts, <a href="/wiki/Apologetics" title="Apologetics">apologies</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hermeneutics" title="Hermeneutics">hermeneutics</a> of a prevailing religious authority. In the case of Early Christianity, this authority was the communion of bishops, and is often referred to by the term "<a href="/wiki/Magisterium" title="Magisterium">Magisterium</a>". The term <i>orthodox</i> was applied<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (November 2017)">when?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> almost as an epithet to a group of Jewish believers who held to pre-Enlightenment understanding of Judaism – now known as <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Judaism" title="Orthodox Judaism">Orthodox Judaism</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a> of Christianity and the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> each consider themselves to be the true heir to Early Christian belief and practice. The antonym of "orthodox" is "<a href="/wiki/Heterodox" class="mw-redirect" title="Heterodox">heterodox</a>", and those adhering to orthodoxy often accuse the heterodox of <a href="/wiki/Apostasy" title="Apostasy">apostasy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Schism_(religion)" class="mw-redirect" title="Schism (religion)">schism</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">heresy</a>.
</p>
<h4><span id="Modernism.2Freform"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Modernism/reform">Modernism/reform</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Modernism/reform">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">April 2019</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> and later the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a> in Europe exhibited varying degrees of <a href="/wiki/Religious_tolerance" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious tolerance">religious tolerance</a> and intolerance towards new and old religious ideas. The <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophes" title="Philosophes">philosophes</a></i> took particular exception to many of the more fantastical claims of religions and directly challenged religious authority and the prevailing beliefs associated with the established churches. In response to the liberalizing political and social movements, some religious groups attempted to integrate Enlightenment ideals of rationality, equality, and individual liberty into their belief systems, especially in the nineteenth and twentieth centuries. <a href="/wiki/Reform_Judaism" title="Reform Judaism">Reform Judaism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Christianity" title="Liberal Christianity">Liberal Christianity</a> offer two examples of such religious associations.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Approaches_to_others">Approaches to others</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Approaches to others">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Adherents of particular religions deal with the differing doctrines and practices espoused by other religions or by other <a href="/wiki/Religious_denomination" title="Religious denomination">religious denominations</a> in a variety of ways.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Exclusivism">Exclusivism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Exclusivism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Religious_exclusivism" title="Religious exclusivism">Religious exclusivism</a></div>
<p>People with exclusivist beliefs typically explain other beliefs either as in error, or as corruptions or counterfeits of the <a href="/wiki/One_true_faith" class="mw-redirect" title="One true faith">true faith</a>. This approach is a fairly consistent feature among smaller <a href="/wiki/New_religious_movement" title="New religious movement">new religious movements</a> that often rely on doctrine that claims a unique <a href="/wiki/Revelation" title="Revelation">revelation</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Founders_of_religious_traditions" class="mw-redirect" title="Founders of religious traditions">founders</a> or <a href="/wiki/Leader" class="mw-redirect" title="Leader">leaders</a>, and considers it a matter of faith that the "correct" religion has a monopoly on truth. All three major <a href="/wiki/Abrahamic_religions" title="Abrahamic religions">Abrahamic monotheistic religions</a> have passages in their holy scriptures that attest to the primacy of the scriptural testimony, and indeed <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">monotheism</a> itself is often<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="This term requires quantification. (June 2018)">quantify</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> vouched<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (November 2017)">by whom?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> as an innovation characterized specifically by its explicit rejection of earlier polytheistic faiths.
</p><p>Some exclusivist faiths incorporate a specific element of <a href="/wiki/Proselytization" class="mw-redirect" title="Proselytization">proselytization</a>. This is a strongly-held belief in the Christian tradition which follows the doctrine of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Commission" title="Great Commission">Great Commission</a>, and is less emphasized by the Islamic faith where the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quranic</a> edict "There shall be no compulsion in religion" (2:256) is often quoted as a justification for toleration of alternative beliefs. The <a href="/wiki/Conversion_to_Judaism" title="Conversion to Judaism">Jewish tradition</a> does not actively seek out converts.
</p><p>Exclusivism correlates with conservative, fundamentalist, and orthodox approaches of many religions, while pluralistic and syncretist approaches either explicitly downplay or reject the exclusivist tendencies within a religion.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Inclusivism">Inclusivism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Inclusivism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>People with <a href="/wiki/Inclusivism" title="Inclusivism">inclusivist</a> beliefs recognize some truth in all faith <a href="/wiki/System" title="System">systems</a>, highlighting agreements and minimizing differences. This attitude is sometimes associated<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (November 2017)">by whom?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> with <a href="/wiki/Interfaith_dialog" class="mw-redirect" title="Interfaith dialog">Interfaith dialogue</a> or with the Christian <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecumenical">Ecumenical</a> movement, though in principle such attempts at pluralism are not necessarily inclusivist and many actors in such interactions (for example, the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a>) still hold to exclusivist dogma while participating in inter-religious organizations.
</p><p>Explicitly inclusivist religions include many that are associated with the <a href="/wiki/New_Age" title="New Age">New Age</a> movement, as well as modern reinterpretations of <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27%C3%AD_Faith" title="Bahá&#39;í Faith">Bahá'í Faith</a> considers it doctrine that there is truth in all faith-systems.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pluralism">Pluralism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Pluralism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religious_pluralism" title="Religious pluralism">Religious pluralism</a></div>
<p>People with <a href="/wiki/Religious_pluralism" title="Religious pluralism">pluralist</a> beliefs make no distinction between faith systems, viewing each one as valid within a particular culture.  Examples include:
</p>
<ul><li>Extracts from the Sri <a href="/wiki/Guru_Granth_Sahib" title="Guru Granth Sahib">Guru Granth Sahib</a> Ji (Sikh Holy Scriptures), "There is only the One Supreme Lord God; there is no other at all" (Pannaa 45). "By His Power the <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedas</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Purana" class="mw-redirect" title="Purana">Puranas</a> exist, and the Holy Scriptures of the Jewish, Christian and Islamic religions. By His Power all deliberations exist." (Pannaa 464). "Some call Him, '<a href="/wiki/Lord_Rama" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Rama">Ram</a>, Ram', and some call Him, 'Khudaa-i'. Some serve Him as 'Gusain', others as 'Allaah'. ||1|| He is the Cause of causes, the Generous Lord. He showers His Grace and Mercy upon us amen." (Pannaa 885).</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Syncretism">Syncretism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Syncretism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">Syncretism</a></div>
<p>People with syncretistic views blend the views of a variety of different religions or traditional beliefs into a unique fusion which suits their particular <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experiences</a> and contexts (<i>see</i> <a href="/wiki/Eclecticism" title="Eclecticism">eclecticism</a>). <a href="/wiki/Unitarian_Universalism" title="Unitarian Universalism">Unitarian Universalism</a> exemplifies a syncretistic faith.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adherence">Adherence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Adherence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God" title="Existence of God">Existence of God</a></div>
<p>Typical reasons for adherence to religion include the following:
</p>
<ul><li>Some see belief in a deity as necessary for <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">moral behavior</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-moore_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moore-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Many<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="This term requires quantification. (November 2017)">quantify</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> people regard religious practices as serene, beautiful, and conducive to religious experiences, which in turn support religious beliefs.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Organized religions promote a sense of <a href="/wiki/Sense_of_community" title="Sense of community">community</a> among their followers, and the moral and cultural common ground of these communities makes them attractive to people with similar <a href="/wiki/Values" class="mw-redirect" title="Values">values</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-repa_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-repa-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Indeed, while religious beliefs and practices are usually connected, some individuals with substantially secular beliefs still participate in religious practices for cultural reasons.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Each religion asserts that it is a means by which its adherents may come into closer contact with the Divine, with Truth, and with <a href="/w/index.php?title=Spiritual_power&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Spiritual power (page does not exist)">spiritual power</a>. They all promise to free adherents from spiritual bondage, and to bring them into spiritual freedom.  It naturally follows that a religion which can free its adherents from deception, sin, and spiritual death will have significant mental-health benefits. <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Maslow" title="Abraham Maslow">Abraham Maslow</a>'s research after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> showed that <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">Holocaust</a> survivors tended to be those who held strong religious beliefs (not necessarily temple attendance, etc.), suggesting that belief helped people cope in extreme circumstances. <a href="/wiki/Humanistic_psychology" title="Humanistic psychology">Humanistic psychology</a> went on to investigate how religious or spiritual identity may have correlations with longer lifespan and better health. The study found that humans may particularly need religious ideas to serve various emotional needs such as the need to feel loved, the need to belong to homogeneous groups, the need for understandable explanations and the need for a guarantee of ultimate justice.  Other factors may involve sense of <a href="/wiki/Purpose" class="mw-redirect" title="Purpose">purpose</a>, sense of <a href="/wiki/Identity_(social_science)" title="Identity (social science)">identity</a>, or a sense of contact with the divine. See also <i><a href="/wiki/Man%27s_Search_for_Meaning" title="Man&#39;s Search for Meaning">Man's Search for Meaning</a></i>, by <a href="/wiki/Viktor_Frankl" title="Viktor Frankl">Viktor Frankl</a>, detailing his experience with the importance of religion in surviving the Holocaust. Critics assert that the very fact that religion was the primary selector for research subjects may have introduced a bias, and that the fact that all subjects were Holocaust survivors may also have had an effect. According to Larson et al. (2000), "[m]ore longitudinal research with better multidimensional measures will help further clarify the roles of these [religious] factors and whether they are beneficial or harmful."<sup id="cite_ref-larson_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-larson-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Psychologist <a href="/wiki/James_Alcock" title="James Alcock">James Alcock</a> also summarizes a number of apparent benefits which reinforce religious belief. These include prayer appearing to account for successful resolution of problems, "a bulwark against existential anxiety and fear of annihilation," an increased sense of control, companionship with one's deity, a source of self-significance, and group identity.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Apostasy">Apostasy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Apostasy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Apostasy" title="Apostasy">Apostasy</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God#Arguments_against_belief_in_God" title="Existence of God">Existence of God §&#160;Arguments against belief in God</a></div>
<p>Typical reasons for rejection of religion include:
</p>
<ul><li>Some people regard certain fundamental doctrines of some religions as illogical, contrary to experience, or unsupported by sufficient <a href="/wiki/Evidence" title="Evidence">evidence</a>; such people may reject one or more religions for those reasons.<sup id="cite_ref-russell_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-russell-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Even some believers may have difficulty accepting particular religious assertions or doctrines. Some people believe the body of evidence available to humans to be insufficient to justify certain religious beliefs. They may thus disagree with religious interpretations of ethics and human purpose, or with various <a href="/wiki/Creation_myth" title="Creation myth">creation myths</a>. This reason has perhaps<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="The material near this tag possibly contains original research. (November 2017)">original research?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> been aggravated by the protestations and empases of some <a href="/wiki/Fundamentalist_Christianity" class="mw-redirect" title="Fundamentalist Christianity">fundamentalist Christians</a>.</li>
<li>Some religions include beliefs that certain groups of people are inferior or sinful and deserve contempt, persecution, or even death, and that non-believers will be punished for their unbelief in an <a href="/wiki/After-life" class="mw-redirect" title="After-life">after-life</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Adherents to a religion may feel antipathy to unbelievers. Numerous examples exist of people of one religion or sect using religion as an excuse to murder people with different religious beliefs. To mention just a few examples:
<ul><li>the slaughter of the <a href="/wiki/Huguenot" class="mw-redirect" title="Huguenot">Huguenots</a> by French <a href="/wiki/Catholics" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholics">Catholics</a> in the sixteenth century</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hindu" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu">Hindus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muslim" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim">Muslims</a> killing each other when <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> separated from India in 1947</li>
<li>the persecution and killing of <a href="/wiki/Shiite" class="mw-redirect" title="Shiite">Shiite</a> Muslims by <a href="/wiki/Sunni" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunni">Sunni</a> Muslims in Iraq</li>
<li>the murder of <a href="/wiki/Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant">Protestants</a> by <a href="/wiki/Catholics" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholics">Catholics</a> and vice versa in <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> (both of these examples in the late twentieth century)</li>
<li>the <a href="/wiki/Israeli%E2%80%93Palestinian_conflict" title="Israeli–Palestinian conflict">Israeli–Palestinian conflict</a> that continues as of 2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> – According to some critics of religion, such beliefs can encourage completely unnecessary conflicts and in some cases even wars. Many <a href="/wiki/Atheist" class="mw-redirect" title="Atheist">atheists</a> believe that, because of this, religion is incompatible with world peace, freedom, civil rights, equality, and good government. On the other hand, most religions perceive atheism as a threat and will vigorously and even violently<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> defend themselves against religious sterilization, making the attempt to remove public religious practices a source of strife.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup></li></ul></li>
<li>Some people may be unable to accept the values that a specific religion promotes and will therefore not join that religion. They may also be unable to accept the proposition that those who do not believe will go to hell or be damned, especially if said nonbelievers are close to the person.</li>
<li>The maintenance of <a href="/wiki/Personal_life" title="Personal life">life</a> and the achievement of <a href="/wiki/Self-esteem" title="Self-esteem">self-esteem</a> require of a person the fullest exercise of reason<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>—but morality (people are taught<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (November 2017)">by whom?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>) rests on and requires faith.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (November 2017)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Systems">Systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ideology" title="Ideology">Ideology</a></div>
<p>An <a href="/wiki/Ideology" title="Ideology">ideology</a> comprises a set of mutually supportive beliefs. The beliefs of any such system can be <a href="/wiki/Religious_belief" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious belief">religious</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophical</a>, <a href="/wiki/Politics" title="Politics">political</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ideology" title="Ideology">ideological</a>, or a combination of these. Philosopher <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Glover" title="Jonathan Glover">Jonathan Glover</a> says that beliefs are always part of a belief system, and that tenanted belief systems are difficult for the tenants to completely revise or reject.<sup id="cite_ref-BitesGlover_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BitesGlover-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="Quotation needed from source to verify. (April 2019)">need quotation to verify</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> This insight has relevance for <a href="/wiki/Inquisitor" title="Inquisitor">inquisitors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Missionaries" class="mw-redirect" title="Missionaries">missionaries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agitprop" title="Agitprop">agitprop</a> groups and <a href="/wiki/Thought_police" class="mw-redirect" title="Thought police">thought-police</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Perspectives">Perspectives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Perspectives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A collective belief is referred to when people speak of what "we" believe when this is not simply elliptical for what "we all" believe.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">Sociologist</a> <a href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Durkheim" title="Émile Durkheim">Émile Durkheim</a> wrote of collective beliefs and proposed that they, like all "<a href="/wiki/Social_fact" title="Social fact">social facts</a>", "inhered in" social <a href="/wiki/Group_(sociology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Group (sociology)">groups</a> as opposed to individual persons. <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Dancy" title="Jonathan Dancy">Jonathan Dancy</a> states that "Durkheim's discussion of collective belief, though suggestive, is relatively obscure".<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">Philosopher</a> <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Gilbert" title="Margaret Gilbert">Margaret Gilbert</a> (1942- ) has offered a related account in terms of the joint commitment of a number of persons as a body to accept a certain belief. According to this account, individuals who together collectively believe something need not personally believe it individually.  Gilbert's work on the topic has stimulated a developing literature among philosophers.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> One question that has arisen is whether and how philosophical accounts of belief in general need to be sensitive to the possibility of collective belief.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Snipa.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Snipa.JPG/240px-Snipa.JPG" decoding="async" width="240" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Snipa.JPG/360px-Snipa.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Snipa.JPG/480px-Snipa.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2816" data-file-height="2112" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Snipa.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Philosopher Jonathan Glover warns that belief systems are like whole boats in the water; it is extremely difficult to alter them all at once (for example, it may be too stressful, or people may maintain their biases without realizing it).<sup id="cite_ref-BitesGlover_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BitesGlover-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Glover" title="Jonathan Glover">Jonathan Glover</a> (1941- ) believes that he and other philosophers ought to play some role in starting dialogues between people with deeply-held, opposing beliefs, especially if there is risk of violence. Glover also believes that philosophy can offer insights about beliefs that would be relevant to such dialogue.
</p><p>Glover suggests that beliefs have to be considered <a href="/wiki/Holistically" class="mw-redirect" title="Holistically">holistically</a>, and that no belief exists in isolation in the mind of the believer. Each belief always implicates and relates to other beliefs.<sup id="cite_ref-BitesGlover_49-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BitesGlover-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Glover provides the example of a patient with an illness who returns to a doctor, but the doctor says that the prescribed medicine is not working. At that point, the patient has a great deal of flexibility in choosing what beliefs to keep or reject: the patient could believe that the doctor is incompetent, that the doctor's assistants made a mistake, that the patient's own body is unique in some unexpected way, that Western medicine is ineffective, or even that Western science is entirely unable to discover truths about ailments.<sup id="cite_ref-BitesGlover_49-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BitesGlover-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Glover maintains that any person can continue to hold any belief if they would really like to<sup id="cite_ref-BitesGlover_49-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BitesGlover-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> (for example, with help from <a href="/wiki/Ad_hoc_hypotheses" class="mw-redirect" title="Ad hoc hypotheses"><i>ad hoc</i> hypotheses</a>). One belief can be held fixed, and other beliefs will be altered around it. Glover warns that some beliefs may not be entirely <a href="/wiki/Explicit_memory" title="Explicit memory">explicitly</a> believed (for example, some people may not realize they have racist belief-systems adopted from their environment as a child). Glover believes that people tend to first realize that beliefs can change, and may be contingent on their upbringing, around age 12 or 15.<sup id="cite_ref-BitesGlover_49-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BitesGlover-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Glover emphasizes that beliefs are difficult to change. He says that one may try to rebuild one's beliefs on more secure foundations (<a href="/wiki/Axioms" class="mw-redirect" title="Axioms">axioms</a>), like building a new house, but warns that this may not be possible. Glover offers the example of <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a>, saying: "[Descartes] starts off with the characteristic beliefs of a 17th-century Frenchman; he then junks the lot, he rebuilds the system, and somehow it looks a lot like the beliefs of a 17th-century Frenchman." To Glover, belief systems are not like houses but are instead like boats. As Glover puts it: "Maybe the whole thing needs rebuilding, but inevitably at any point you have to keep enough of it intact to keep floating."<sup id="cite_ref-BitesGlover_49-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BitesGlover-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Glover's final message is that if people talk about their beliefs, they may find more deep, relevant, philosophical ways in which they disagree (e.g., less obvious beliefs, or more-deeply-held beliefs). Glover thinks that people often manage to find agreements and consensus through philosophy. He says that at the very least, if people do not convert each other, they will hold their own beliefs more <a href="/wiki/Open-mindedness" title="Open-mindedness">openmindedly</a> and will be less likely to go to war over conflicting beliefs.<sup id="cite_ref-BitesGlover_49-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BitesGlover-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GloverBeliefs_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GloverBeliefs-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The British philosopher <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Law" title="Stephen Law">Stephen Law</a> (1960-) has described some belief systems (including belief in <a href="/wiki/Homeopathy" title="Homeopathy">homeopathy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Psychic_powers" class="mw-redirect" title="Psychic powers">psychic powers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Alien_abduction" title="Alien abduction">alien abduction</a>) as <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/claptrap" class="extiw" title="wikt:claptrap">"claptrap"</a> and says that such belief-systems can "draw people in and hold them captive so they become willing slaves of claptrap [...] if you get sucked in, it can be extremely difficult to think your way clear again".<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li style="display:table-row"><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Psi2.svg/28px-Psi2.svg.png" decoding="async" width="28" height="28" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Psi2.svg/42px-Psi2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Psi2.svg/56px-Psi2.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="100" data-file-height="100" /></span><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle"><a href="/wiki/Portal:Psychology" title="Portal:Psychology">Psychology portal</a></span></li>
<li style="display:table-row"><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/File:Social_sciences.svg" class="image"><img alt="icon" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Social_sciences.svg/32px-Social_sciences.svg.png" decoding="async" width="32" height="28" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Social_sciences.svg/48px-Social_sciences.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Social_sciences.svg/64px-Social_sciences.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="139" data-file-height="122" /></a></span><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle"><a href="/wiki/Portal:Society" title="Portal:Society">Society portal</a></span></li></ul></div>
<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 15em; -webkit-column-width: 15em; column-width: 15em;">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Alief_(belief)" class="mw-redirect" title="Alief (belief)">Alief</a></li>

<li><a href="/wiki/Doxastic_logic" title="Doxastic logic">Doxastic logic</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Expectation_(epistemic)" title="Expectation (epistemic)">Expectation (epistemic)</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Folk_psychology" title="Folk psychology">Folk psychology</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Idea" title="Idea">Idea</a></li>

<li id="cite_note-russell-45"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-russell_45-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For example: <cite class="citation web"><a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Russell" title="Bertrand Russell">Russell, Bertrand</a> (1927). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20061119081311/http://www.positiveatheism.org/hist/russell0.htm">"Why I am Not a Christian"</a>. <li id="cite_note-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-46">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For example, some <a href="/wiki/Muslim" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim">Muslims</a> believe that women are inferior to men. Some <a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christians</a> share this belief. At the time of the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> of 1861–1865, many Southerners used passages from the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a> to justify race-based <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a>. Certain campaigners have used the Christian religion as a reason to persecute and to deny the rights of homosexuals, on the basis that the Christian biblical God disapproves of homosexuality, and by implication of homosexuals. Compare <a rel="nofollow" class="external free" href="http://www.godhatesfags.com">http://www.godhatesfags.com</a></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-47"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-47">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Beauchamp, Philip (pseudonym of Jeremy Bentham) <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=qiRQAAAAIAAJ&amp;printsec=frontcover"><i>Analysis of the Influence of Natural Religion on the Temporal Happiness of Mankind</i></a>, 1822, R. Carlile, London, at page 76: "Of all human antipathies, that which the believer in a God bears to the unbeliever is the fullest, the most unqualified, and the most universal"</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-48">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Faith is the commitment of one's consciousness to beliefs for which one has no sensory evidence or rational proof. When a person rejects reason as their standard of judgment, only one alternative standard allegedly remains to them: feelings. A mystic is a person who treats feelings as tools of cognition. Faith is the equation of feeling with knowledge. To practice the "virtue" of faith, one must (we are told) be willing to suspend one's sight and one's judgment; one must be willing to live with the unintelligible, with that which cannot be conceptualized or integrated into the rest of one's knowledge, and to induce a trance like illusion of understanding. One must allegedly be willing to repress one's critical faculty and hold it as one's guilt; one must be willing to drown any questions that rise in protest—to strangle any trust of <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">reason</a> convulsively seeking to assert its proper function as the protector of one's life and cognitive integrity. The presumed human need for self-esteem entails the need for a sense of control over reality—but no control is possible in a universe which, by one's own concession, contains the supernatural, the miraculous and the causeless, a universe in which one is at the mercy of ghosts and demons, in which one must deal, not with the unknown, but with the unknowable; no control is possible if a person proposes, but a ghost disposes; no control is possible if the universe is a haunted house. A person's life and self-esteem require that the object and concern of his or her consciousness be reality and this earth—but morality, people are taught, consists of scorning this earth and the world available to sensory perception, and of contemplating, instead, a "different" and "higher" reality, a realm inaccessible to reason and incommunicable in language, but attainable by revelation, by special dialectical processes, by that superior state of intellectual lucidity known to Zen-Buddhists as "No-Mind," or by death. A person's life and self-esteem require that this person take pride in their power to think, pride in their power to live—but morality, people are taught, holds pride, and specifically intellectual pride, as the gravest of sins. Virtue begins, people are taught, with humility: with the recognition of the helplessness, the smallness, the impotence of one's mind. A person's life and self-esteem purportedly require the person to be loyal to their values, loyal to their mind and its judgments, loyal to their life—but the essence of morality, people are taught, consists of self-sacrifice: the sacrifice of one's mind to some higher authority, and the sacrifice of one's values to whoever may claim to require it. A sacrifice, it is necessary to remember, means the surrender of a higher value in favor of a lower value or of a nonvalue. If one gives up that which one does not value in order to obtain that which one does value—or if one gives up a lesser value in order to  obtain a greater one—this is not a sacrifice, but a gain. Remember further that all of a person's values allegedly exist in a hierarchy; people value some things more than others; and, to the extent that a person is rational, the hierarchical order of the person's values is rational: that is, the person values things in proportion to their importance in serving this person's life and well-being. That which is inimical to their life and well-being, that which is inimical to their nature and needs as a living being, the person disvalues. Conversely, one of the characteristics of mental illness is a distorted value structure; the neurotic does not value things according to their objective merit, in relation to the person's nature and needs; they frequently value the very things that will lead them to self-destruction. Judged by objective standards, they are engaged in a chronic process of self-sacrifice. But if sacrifice is a virtue, it is not the neurotic but the rational person who must be “cured”. They must learn to do violence to their own rational judgment—to reverse the order of their value hierarchy—to surrender that which their mind has chosen as the good—to turn against and invalidate their own consciousness.<cite class="citation book">Waldau, Paul (2001). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=VMvWXaOM1m0C"><i>The Specter of Speciesism: Buddhist and Christian Views of Animals (American Academy of Religion Books)</i></a>. Oxford University Press, US. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-514571-7" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19-514571-7"><bdi>978-0-19-514571-7</bdi></a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Specter+of+Speciesism%3A+Buddhist+and+Christian+Views+of+Animals+%28American+Academy+of+Religion+Books%29&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press%2C+US&amp;rft.date=2001&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-19-514571-7&amp;rft.aulast=Waldau&amp;rft.aufirst=Paul&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DVMvWXaOM1m0C&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABelief" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-BitesGlover-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-BitesGlover_49-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-BitesGlover_49-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-BitesGlover_49-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-BitesGlover_49-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-BitesGlover_49-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-BitesGlover_49-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-BitesGlover_49-6"><sup><i><b>g</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-BitesGlover_49-7"><sup><i><b>h</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://philosophybites.com/2011/10/jonathan-glover-on-systems-of-belief.html">"Jonathan Glover on systems of belief", Philosophy Bites Podcast, Oct 9 2011</a></span>

<li id="cite_note-52"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-52">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">

<li id="cite_note-54"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-54">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">

Belief in homeopathy, psychic powers, alien abductions – these are examples of intellectual black holes. As you approach them, you need to be on your guard because if you get sucked in, it can be extremely difficult to think your way clear again."</span>

</ol></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_reading">Further reading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Further reading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Robert_Audi" title="Robert Audi">Robert Audi</a>. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/stable/2215473">"Dispositional Beliefs and Dispositions to Believe"</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/No%C3%BBs" title="Noûs">Noûs</a></i>, Vol. 28, No. 4 (Dec., 1994), pp.&#160;419–434.  <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/><a href="/wiki/OCLC" title="OCLC">OCLC</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.worldcat.org/oclc/481484099">481484099</a></li>
<li>Coleman III, T., Jong, J., &amp; van Mulukom, V. (2018). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://booksandjournals.brillonline.com/content/journals/10.1163/18758185-01503001">Introduction to the Special Issue: What are Religious Beliefs?</a>. Contemporary Pragmatism, 15(3), 279–283. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1163%2F18758185-01503001">10.1163/18758185-01503001</a></li>
<li>Elisa Järnefelt, <i>Created by Some Being: Theoretical and Empirical Exploration of Adults' Automatic and Reflective Beliefs about the Origin of Natural Phenomena.</i> Diss. University of Helsinki, 2013. <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/><a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-952-10-9416-3" title="Special:BookSources/978-952-10-9416-3">978-952-10-9416-3</a>.</li>
<li>Fred Leavitt, "Dancing with Absurdity: Your Most Cherished Beliefs (and All Your Others) are Probably Wrong.  Peter Lang, 2015.</li>
<li>J. Leicester,  "What beliefs are made from". Sharjah, UAE: Bentham Science Publishers, 2016.</li></ul>
